#### Labeled and unlabeled data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=1)** - When you think about machine learning, the key is to focus on the term learning.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=5)** What does it mean for your machine to learn?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=8)** What strategies can you use to learn something new?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=11)** How can you take the strategies and apply them to machines?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=16)** Imagine you wanted to learn how to play chess.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=18)** You could do this a couple of different ways.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=20)** You could hire a chess tutor, and they would introduce you to some of the different chess pieces.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=25)** Then they'd show you how to move them around the board.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=28)** You could practice by playing against your tutor.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=30)** Then they would supervise your moves and help you when you made a mistake.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=35)** If you couldn't find a tutor, you could also go to public parks.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=39)** There, you'd watch people play.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=40)** You couldn't ask them questions, you'd just quietly watch and learn.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=45)** You'd have to figure out chess just by watching the games on your own.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=49)** If you did this long enough, you'd probably understand the game.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=53)** You might not know the names of the chess pieces, but you'd understand the moves and strategies after hundreds of hours of observation.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=60)** These two strategies are very similar to how a machine learns.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=65)** The system could do something called supervised learning.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=68)** Here, a data scientist acts like a tutor for the machine.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=73)** They show the machine the correct answers and then let the system train itself to get better at the game.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=79)** The system could also do unsupervised learning.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=81)** Here you just have the machine make all the observations on its own.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=85)** The system might not know all the different names and labels, but it'll figure out their way to learn from the data.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=92)** As you can imagine, these two approaches have their own strengths and weaknesses.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=96)** For supervised learning, the system needs to have a knowledgeable tutor.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=100)** There must be someone that knows a lot about chess that can show the system how to play the game.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=105)** With unsupervised learning, the system needs to have access to a lot of data.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=109)** That's the only way to see the patterns.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=112)** The system might not be able to go to a public park and watch hundreds of people play.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=117)** It also depends a little bit on who it watches.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=120)** You need it to watch people who are good players.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=123)** As you can imagine, these techniques are used for much more than just playing chess.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=128)** Companies use these techniques to get valuable insights about their customer.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=132)** With supervised learning, a company like Amazon might identify a thousand customers who spend a lot of time shopping on their website.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=140)** The company can then label these customers as high spenders.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=144)** Then it would have the machine learning system look through the customer to find patterns that make them high spenders.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=151)** Now, for unsupervised learning, a machine learning system could be given access to all of Amazon's customer data.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=157)** Here, the system might find its own patterns in the data.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=161)** Maybe somebody who buys chessboards are much more likely to buy an expensive kitchen appliance.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=167)** Then Amazon could use that data to advertise.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=170)** If you use Amazon, you may have noticed that sometimes they advertise products that seem completely unrelated to what you're looking for, but it's still something you're interested in buying.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/labeled-and-unlabeled-data?u=76281980&t=180)** Both techniques have their own strengths, and yet each one can give you incredibly useful insights.

#### Massive datasets
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=1)** - If you've ever worked as a product manager or a software developer, then you know that applications need very explicit instructions.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=9)** Every time you open Microsoft Windows or open an app on your iPhone, you're benefiting from a programmer coding the input and output.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=19)** But we've seen that this type of programming doesn't work well with artificial intelligence.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=24)** There's too many combinations to tie every input to an output.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=29)** In these cases, you need a programming model that allows the machine to learn.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=33)** You also must give the machine some ability to respond to feedback.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=38)** Imagine you're creating a program that detects Spam messages.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=42)** These messages are filled with unwanted advertisements or viruses.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=47)** You could easily program a word filter that deletes messages with common Spam words.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=53)** If you frequently get messages on entering contests, you can filter words like "Gold," "Lottery" or "Winner."
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=60)** So you can program that if the message contains "Gold," then treat the message as Spam.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=67)** But complex challenges don't work well when you're limited to programmed instructions.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=72)** That's why machine learning switches this around.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=75)** Instead of inputting instructions, you're inputting data.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=80)** Instead of a program response, you let the machine learn from the patterns it identifies.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=86)** You can start with supervised machine learning.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=89)** Here, you need to split your data into a training set and a test set.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=94)** The training data is a smaller chunk of data that the machine uses to learn.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=99)** The system will use machine learning algorithms.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=102)** These algorithms rely on statistics.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=105)** You'll see a bunch of these machine learning algorithms later on.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=109)** These algorithms help the machine find relationships within the data.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=113)** So the machine uses an algorithm that if the email message contains the word "Lucky," "Winner" or "Congratulations," then it's 50% more likely to be Spam.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=123)** Then once the algorithm is accurate enough, you can use what the system learned on a larger test dataset.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=130)** This test data is usually many times larger than the data that the machine used to train.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=136)** Let's think about how machine learning might work with our Spam detection program.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=140)** We'll set aside 10,000 email messages for our training set.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=144)** We'll use it to build our model.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=146)** This training data has 9,000 regular messages and 1,000 messages labeled as Spam.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=153)** We'll also set aside a million messages for our test data.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=157)** This test data is unlabeled.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=159)** That means that it's unlike our training set.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=161)** No one has correctly labeled any of the messages as Spam.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=165)** That would be a lot of work for a million messages.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=168)** Your training data is then used to let the machine identify the Spam messages.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=174)** Then once the machine learning algorithm gets close to identifying the 1,000 Spam messages, you'll try it on the larger test dataset.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=183)** Once you're satisfied, that will be your initial data model.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=188)** Now, this machine learning algorithm only has two options.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=191)** Is that a regular message or a Spam message?
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=194)** That's why this is called a Binary Classification Challenge.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=199)** You'll only need to classify your email message into one or two groups.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=203)** This is one of the most common uses for machine learning.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=207)** The key thing to remember is that machine learning algorithms use statistics to find patterns in your data.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/massive-datasets?u=76281980&t=213)** Once your machine identifies these patterns, it can then classify your data based on what it's learned.

#### Data models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - If you work with a machine learning team, one phrase you'll hear is "We're working to improve the data model."
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=6)** In fact, these teams usually put a very high priority on working to improve the data model.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=12)** So what is a data model?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=14)** The best way to think of a data model is an abstraction of what the AI system learned from all that training and data.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=21)** That's why the data model is never finished.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=24)** A good machine learning team will push their model to become more accurate.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=29)** So if it's 99% accurate, the team will push it to become 99.9% accurate, then 99.999% accurate.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=39)** The system needs to continue to learn until it almost always guesses correctly.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=45)** It might sound strange, but you also use data models.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=49)** When you get behind the wheel of a friend's car, you know how to start the engine, even though you've never driven the car before.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=55)** If you see a bird in the sky, you'll recognize it as a bird, even if you've never seen the species before.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=60)** That's because, throughout your life, you've created data models to help you understand the world.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=66)** You've taken a lot of data, and then you have extracted it down to core concepts.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=72)** That way, you can apply these concepts to something new.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=75)** The more data you've processed, the more accurate you'll be at making guesses.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=80)** So if you've seen thousands of species of birds, there's a pretty good chance that you'll guess the correct species.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=86)** The more cars you've driven, the better you'll be at driving a new car.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=90)** In a sense, your data model is what you've learned from all that training and data.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=95)** Data models are important to understand when comparing predictive and generative artificial intelligence.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=102)** Data models are mostly used for predictive AI.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=106)** In fact, most of the machine learning algorithms, that you'll see later, are used for data models, so you'll only use them to make predictions.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=116)** That's because predictive systems are trying to get better at making specific guesses.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=122)** You'll be creating a data model to understand that one data set.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=126)** It's the same way that you would use a different data model to drive a car than would use to identify a bird.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=133)** Each of these requires training on a different model.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=137)** So, if you drive a lot of cars, you won't be any better at identifying birds.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=141)** You need to train yourself on bird species, but that won't help you be a better driver.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=146)** But remember that generative AI is much more flexible, but it also needs more data.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=152)** Think about if you ask a generative AI chatbot a complex question.

>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=163)** The Gen AI system needs to know a lot about birds, cars, roads, and even television to be able to generate that story.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=171)** Data models are still the core of AI in most organizations.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=175)** That's because predictive AI gives you a lot of power, but not a lot of flexibility.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/data-models?u=76281980&t=180)** Later, you'll see a lot more flexible model that you can use for newer generative artificial intelligence.

#### Cluster data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=1)** - Classifying your data doesn't fit every challenge.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=3)** For starters, the system won't always have access to massive amounts of labeled data.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=9)** So, sometimes you want to have the system create its own data clusters.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=13)** Clusters are when the machine uses unsupervised learning to create its own groups of data.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=19)** If you've ever bought something online, you might notice that the store will include something called, "Frequently bought together."
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=25)** Maybe you're buying a computer mouse and it recommends a keyboard.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=28)** This is a very powerful feature, and it helps customers find what they need and increases sales for the company.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=35)** This is an example of a system using unsupervised learning to create clusters based on what it sees in your purchasing history.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=43)** The big difference between clustering and classifying is whether you're working with human-created categories or machine-created groups.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=53)** In general, if you're using supervised learning, you're classifying.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=57)** And if you're using unsupervised learning, then you're clustering.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=62)** Think of it this way.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=63)** Every Halloween, my son goes trick-or-treating.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=66)** This is when kids wear costumes and go around the neighborhood to get candy.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=70)** At the end of the evening, my son comes home with hundreds of little pieces of candy.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=75)** The first thing he wants to do is classify the candy by what he likes the best.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=80)** Now in the past, my son has benefited from my experience.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=84)** I've been able to supervise his learning.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=86)** I can help him create output categories for the candy, such as chocolate, peanut butter, mints, and gummies.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=94)** Then he does his best to classify some of the unknown candies into these categories.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=99)** This would be the same as supervised machine learning.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=103)** Now, he also has grandparents that live in a different country.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=106)** They feel bad that they can't participate in trick-or-treating.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=109)** So, each year they send a bag of Serbian candy.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=113)** With this bag, we can't use supervised learning because it's unlabeled.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=118)** Neither he or I have ever seen the candy before and the wrappers are in Cyrillic.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=123)** So in this case, he does a form of unsupervised learning.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=127)** He looks at the bag and creates his own clusters.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=130)** He does this based on his own study of the data.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=134)** He might create a cluster based on the candy size or the color.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=138)** In fact, one year he created a cluster that I'd never considered.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=141)** It was a small cluster that he called, "perfume candy", made from roses and orange blossoms.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=148)** This is a key part of unsupervised learning.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=150)** Like my son, the machine studies the data and then comes up with its own clusters.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=156)** One of the biggest advantages of clustering is that there's a lot more unlabeled data.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=161)** There's a lot of candy in the world that I've never seen, so I'm not going to be able to create these output categories.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=168)** There are also many ways that you might want to use machine learning to create clusters.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=173)** You might want to have your machine learning algorithms cluster your customers.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=176)** Then a human can go through and see if there's any patterns.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=180)** At first glance, these clusters might not seem important.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=183)** They may even seem trivial.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=184)** But keep in mind that some of the largest companies are built around creating data clusters.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=189)** Companies like Amazon, Netflix, and Twitter are all using machine learning to cluster your friends, your search history, and buying habits.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/cluster-data?u=76281980&t=199)** These systems see patterns that would be impossible for a human to create.

#### Reinforcement learning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=1)** - Online music is close to a $30 billion industry.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=4)** And if you think about it, it's kind of a strange business.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=7)** A lot of times you can buy the same songs from Apple Music, Spotify, or Tidal.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=13)** So, why would you want to sign up for one service over the other?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=16)** For most people, this has to do with the power of their recommendation system.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=21)** A lot of these systems started off by using unsupervised machine learning.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=25)** They would recommend songs the same way that online retailers would say things were frequently bought together.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=31)** But the best music libraries don't just want you to buy things together.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=34)** Many of them want you to discover something new.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=36)** So for that, you have to use a different form of machine learning.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=41)** This form of machine learning is called reinforcement learning.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=44)** These are machine learning algorithms that use rewards as a way to give the system incentive to find new patterns.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=53)** A few years ago, Google used reinforcement learning to teach artificial intelligence systems how to play video games.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=60)** Their AI systems beat expert players at Pong, Atari, and even more modern video games.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=66)** But reinforcement learning can do much more than just play video games.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=69)** These systems can improve over time by setting a series of goals and rewards.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=76)** Think of it this way.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=77)** Spotify Discover Weekly compares your favorite songs with a bunch of related songs.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=82)** The machine learning algorithm tracks every time you click and play a song.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=87)** It also keeps track of how long you listen.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=89)** The data scientists design the algorithm so that every time you click a related song, it gets a tiny digital reward.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=97)** It's almost like money for the machine learning system.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=100)** It gets this reward coin when you click on the recommended song.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=104)** Then the longer you listen, the more the reward increases.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=109)** So, it gets a reward coin for every minute that you listen to the song.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=113)** These algorithms often use something called Q-Learning.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=117)** This type of reinforcement learning helps create more sophisticated rewards.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=122)** In Q-Learning, there are set environments or states.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=125)** There are also possible actions that can respond to the states.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=129)** In Q-Learning, you want the machine to improve the quality of the outcome, which is represented by the letter Q.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=137)** In this case, you'd start with a Q of zero.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=140)** Then you'd have the machine learn the actions that improve its conditions.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=145)** The state of Q would go up each time you clicked a song.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=148)** You can almost think of Q-Learning like the system's bank account.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=152)** It can earn a bunch of coins and then see the balance grow.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=156)** Once the reward system is set up, it'll look for patterns to try and increase its Q-Learning bank account.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=163)** So, if it sees a pattern where people who like one song might listen to another, it'll exploit that and make sure to put that song on your Discover Weekly.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=172)** Reinforcement learning systems work best when your organization wants to do more than just cluster items that are frequently bought together.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=181)** With reinforcement learning, your organization can build a system that thinks creatively about what your customer can discover.

>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=36)** The key thing to remember is that each of these algorithms are like a chef's kitchen tool.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=41)** Spoons are used for stirring and knives are used for cutting, but sometimes chefs can find new creative uses for that tool.

>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=60)** They started out using supervised machine learning to classify the customers into two different groups.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=66)** Remember, this is called binary classification.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=69)** The first group of customers use promotions and the second group never used them.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=74)** Then they use machine learning algorithms to train the system on this binary classification.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=79)** Once they classified their customers into these two groups, they used unsupervised machine learning.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=85)** They wanted to see if they could learn something about their customers who used promotions, so they let the machine create clusters of this specific customer.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=94)** Remember that unsupervised machine learning lets the system create its own clusters based on the patterns that it sees in the data.

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/common-algorithms?u=76281980&t=101)** It's the same way that my son found clusters in unknown candy.

>

>

#### K-nearest neighbor
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=1)** - In machine learning, one of the best ways to learn more about your data is by classifying it with what you already know.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=7)** Think of it this way.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=8)** When I was younger, I worked for an animal shelter in Chicago.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=12)** One of the most difficult jobs was classifying the breed of incoming dogs.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=17)** There are hundreds of different dog breeds and most dogs are mixed.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=22)** Each time we got a new dog, we would hold it up to the dogs whose breed we already knew.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=27)** Then we'd look at some of the features.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=29)** Maybe it was the shape of their face or the color of their hair.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=32)** In a sense, the shelter was classifying the unknown dog by looking at its nearest neighbor.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=39)** Of course, it's not easy to tell whether a dog was a Boston Terrier or a French Bulldog.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=44)** The closer the match, the more likely it was to be classified.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=48)** Another way to look at it is we were trying to minimize the distance between the unknown dog and the known breeds.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=55)** If the features were closely matched, then there was a short distance between the unknown dog and its nearest neighbor.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=63)** A very common supervised machine learning algorithm for multi-class classification is called K Nearest Neighbor.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=70)** The algorithm plots new data and compares it to the data that you already have.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=75)** Multi-class classification is different from binary classification because there are more than two dog breeds.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=82)** Minimizing the distance is a key part of K Nearest Neighbor.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=86)** The closer you are to your nearest neighbors, the more likely you are to be accurate.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=91)** The most common way to do that is through something called Euclidean Distance.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=95)** This is a mathematical formula that can help see the distance between data points.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=101)** Now imagine you had millions of dogs and you wanted to classify them based on their breed.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=106)** To start out, you might want to create two key features.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=110)** These will help you classify the dogs that share the same breed.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=113)** These are often called classification predictors, so let's use their weight and the length of their hair.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=120)** Then we'll take these two features and put them on an XY axis diagram.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=125)** This is the same diagram that you used in geometry in school.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=129)** Let's put the length of their hair along the Y-axis and their weight along the X-axis.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=135)** Now take 1000 labeled dogs for the training set.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=139)** This will be like the shelter dogs where we already knew the breed.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=142)** We'll put them on the graph based on their weight and hair length.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=146)** Now let's take our unknown dog and put it on the chart.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=149)** You can see that it's not matched with another dog, but it has a bunch of neighbors.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=154)** Let's say we use a K of five.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=156)** That means that we'd want to put a circle around our unclassified dog and its five nearest neighbors.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=163)** We can see if the distance of the other dogs is shorter, will get a much more accurate classification.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=169)** Now let's look at the five nearest neighbors.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=172)** You'll see that three of them are shepherds and two of them are huskies.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=176)** You can be somewhat confident to classify your unknown dog as a Shepherd.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=181)** There's also a reasonable chance that it's a Husky.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=184)** K Nearest Neighbor is a very common and powerful machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=188)** That's because it can do more than just classify dogs.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-nearest-neighbor?u=76281980&t=191)** In fact, it's commonly used in finance to look for the best stocks and even predict future performance.

#### K-means clustering
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=1)** - Another common machine learning algorithm is k-means clustering.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=4)** K-means clustering is an unsupervised machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=8)** It's used to create clusters based on what your machine sees in the data.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's go back to the animal shelter in Chicago.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=16)** The shelter used to have a large social room where the dogs got together, sniffed and played.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=22)** The dogs had their group of friends, and they played and hung out together.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=26)** Each time they had a social hour, they would self-organize into these social groups.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=32)** Now, imagine that the shelter was closing and all the dogs were going to be distributed to three different shelters.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=38)** To make it easier for the dogs, the organizers decided to cluster the dogs based on their friend groups.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=44)** So the shelter created three clusters.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=48)** That means the k in k means equal three, because they wanted to divide the groups into three clusters.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=55)** To start, the machine put a red, yellow, or blue-colored collar on three random dogs.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=62)** Each color represented a potential cluster based on their social group.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=67)** These would be your three centroid dogs.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=70)** Each of the centroid dogs would look at the mean or average distance between it itself and all of the surrounding dogs.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=78)** Then the machine would put the same-colored collar on the dogs that were closest to a centroid.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=84)** Since these centroid dogs were selected randomly, there was a pretty good chance that they wouldn't really have any good clusters.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=91)** Maybe all three centroid dogs were in the same social group.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=94)** If that happened, then most of the dogs would be far away from their nearest centroid, so it would redistribute collars until the algorithm could find a good centroid dog.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=105)** The machine would try over and over again until it picked the best centroid, and it might even do this one cluster at a time.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=112)** At the end of each iteration, the machine learning algorithm checked the variance between each dog and the centroid.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=119)** Once there was a good centroid, then it would put the same-colored collars on the friends of the dogs in each cluster.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=126)** Now, keep in mind that the dogs themselves did not cluster into three groups.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=131)** There might be seven or eight different social groups, but there were only three shelters, so the algorithm had to do its best to cluster the dogs' natural social grouping.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=141)** The algorithm also had to consider very social dogs that jumped from group to group.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=145)** If the dogs were moving around too much, then it'd be difficult to form real clusters.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=150)** Another challenge with k-means is it can be very sensitive to outliers.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=154)** So if you had a dog that wasn't hanging out with other dogs, it would still have to be clustered into one of these three groups.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=161)** Essentially, the dog would be forced to find friends.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=165)** Organizing dogs into three clusters for three different shelters is probably not a problem you'll run into every day.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=171)** But k-means clustering is one of the most popular machine learning algorithms.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=175)** One of the more interesting applications is when retailers use clustering to decide who gets promotions.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=181)** They might have the system create three clusters to find loyal customers, regular customers, and lowest-price shoppers.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=188)** Then they'll create strategies to elevate regular customers into loyal customers.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=193)** Many organizations are looking for better ways to cluster together their customers.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=197)** If they can get all of their loyal customers into one cluster, then they can really improve their business.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=203)** K-means clustering is a good way for the system to cluster people or things by looking at hundreds of different variables.

#### Regression

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=1)** - I once worked for a company that sold vehicles online.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=3)** Each time they sent a customer to an auto dealership, the company earned a referral fee.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=9)** For them, it was always about looking at trends in auto sales.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=13)** People were much more likely to buy convertibles and sports cars in the spring and summer.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=18)** Others were more likely to buy trucks and SUVs in the fall and winter.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=23)** So when customers visited the website, they had seasonal promotions for what people wanted to buy.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=29)** One of the tools they used was regression analysis.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=33)** Regression analysis is a supervised machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=36)** It looks at the relationship between predictors and the outcome.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=40)** Sometimes you'll hear predictors called input variables, independent variables, or even regressors.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=47)** Regression analysis is a supervised machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=51)** You're taking the training data and labeling the correct output.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=55)** Then you're using the labeled data with the test data.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=59)** The best way to think about regression is to imagine trends.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=62)** As the weather gets warmer, people are more likely to buy convertibles.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=66)** As the weather gets cooler, people are more likely to buy trucks and SUVs.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=70)** Regression analysis doesn't tell you why people do these things.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=74)** This is for data scientists and business analysts to figure out.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=78)** It just tells you that these things are happening.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=81)** Machine learning regression algorithms work in a similar way.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=85)** Once you have your training data, you make a prediction, then see how close you are to the outcome.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=91)** Then you repeat over and over again until the system makes the most accurate prediction.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=97)** In this case, the data science team thought that the change of seasons would be a great predictor for the sale of some vehicles.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=104)** So they put the months as a predictor.
>

>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=115)** Remember that this is the simple chart that you learned from geometry.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=118)** Along the x or bottom axis, they listed the names of the month, and along the y or top axis, they put the sales by vehicle type.

> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=128)** Then they looked at the trend line.

>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=139)** For trucks and SUVs, it was the opposite.

> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=147)** As you can imagine, regression can be enormously powerful for organizations.

>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=164)** People buy more Pop-Tarts in the summer, and they buy more milk and cheese in the winter.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=168)** They want to make sure that the shelves are filled with these items based on predicted buying trends.

> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=175)** One interesting thing about regression analysis is that there's some question about it's true machine learning.

> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=181)** That makes sense because the system isn't learning anything new.

> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=188)** Either way, regression is a very popular way for businesses to predict future behavior, and these trends are everywhere.

> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/regression?u=76281980&t=203)** What you learn might surprise you.

#### Naive Bayes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've seen that sometimes you can classify items based on the nearest neighbor.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=4)** You can also classify based on trends in the data, but sometimes you want to classify items based on many features in the data.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=13)** For that, you can use something called Naive Bayes.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=17)** Naive Bayes is one of the most popular machine learning algorithms.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=21)** It's naive because it assumes all the predictors are independent from one another.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=27)** So let's go back to our animal shelter.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=29)** Imagine we want to classify all the dogs based on their breeds.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=33)** Let's look at the problem using a Naive Bayes machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=38)** To start, let's create three classes of dog breeds.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=41)** We'll use terrier, hounds, and sport dogs.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=45)** Now, for each of these classes, we'll use three features as predictors.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=50)** Let's use hair length, height, and weight.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=54)** Remember that some of these predictors will be closely auto correlated.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=59)** A tall dog is more likely to weigh more, but Naive Bayes considers each one of these predictors independently.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=66)** Remember, that's why it's called naive.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=69)** Once you have your classes and predictors set up, then the Naive Bayes will do something called class predictor probability.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=77)** This is when it looks at each one of the predictors and creates a probability that the dog belongs in this class.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=84)** So let's see what happens when we try to identify an unknown dog.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=88)** The first predictor we look at is hair length.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=90)** The machine learning algorithm checks the probability of a dog with this hair length belonging in the three breeds.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=98)** It finds that a dog with this hair length has a 40% chance of being a terrier, a 10% chance of being a hound, and a 50% chance that it's a sport dog.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=109)** The next thing you check is the unknown dog's height.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=112)** It looks at this predictor independently and tries to calculate the class predictor probability.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=119)** So it looks at the training data and finds that there's a 20% chance that it's a terrier, a 10% chance it's a hound, and a 70% chance that it's a sport dog.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=131)** The final thing you want to check is the unknown dog's weight.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=134)** This might seem like a strange predictor because it's closely related to height, but remember that Naive Bayes is evaluating the probability of each predictor independently.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=146)** It looks at the training data and finds that there's a 10% chance that it's a terrier, a 5% chance that it's a hound, and an 85% chance that it's a sports dog.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=156)** So now you have this table with the unknown dog's class predictor probability.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=161)** If you look at it, you can see that the dog is probably a sport dog.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=165)** As you can imagine, organizations can use Naive Bayes to do much more than just classify dog breeds.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=171)** Banks use it to check for fraud.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=173)** They look at each banking predictor independently and then measure the likelihood that it's fraud.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=179)** Then they use a class predictor probability to classify the transaction.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=184)** Cybersecurity firms also use Naive Bayes to look for securities threats.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=189)** It looks at each threat predictor independently, and then flags items for security review.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=196)** The key thing is that because Naive Bayes makes so few assumptions, it can look in an enormous amount of predictors.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/naive-bayes?u=76281980&t=204)** Often these extra predictors make it much more accurate when classifying your data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Analogies:** imagine (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1)

>

>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=52)** It's when a group performs together.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=54)** It's the same thing with machine learning algorithms.
>
