>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=60)** The most popular is bagging and stacking.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=63)** Bagging is when you use several versions of the same machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=68)** Stacking is when you use several different machine learning algorithms, then you stack them on top of one another.
>

>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=82)** You'd be surprised how much retailers earn by selling something just a few minutes before your checkout.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=87)** So this was a big challenge, and they wanted to create an ensemble of machine learning algorithms.

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=101)** Then they'd see if they could improve their accuracy.

>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=108)** So they could get data samples from random stores, then use K nearest neighbor to classify those data sets separately.

>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=126)** In a sense, they were averaging out the insights to see if they could come up with a more accurate result.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=132)** The retailer could also try boosting.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=135)** Here, instead of averaging the insights together, they'd boost the results step-by-step.
>

>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=148)** Then they could use K nearest neighbor to see what's often bought with the hammer.
>

>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=164)** For that, we might want to use something like naive bayes.

> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/select-the-best-algorithm?u=76281980&t=168)** Remember that naive bayes is naive because it doesn't assume that predictors are correlated.

#### Follow the data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the old movie, "All the President's Men," the top informant of the Nixon scandal met in a parking lot and said, "Follow the money."
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=9)** Only by following the money could the reporter find the truth.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=13)** Like the reporter, machine learning algorithms must follow the data to get to the truth, but that's easier said than done.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=21)** In fact, one of the biggest challenges in machine learning is balancing the bias and the variance.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=28)** Bias is the gap between the predicted value and the actual outcome.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=33)** Let's say that you were playing dice and predicted that you would roll 5 three times, but you rolled 4 three times.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=40)** Then your prediction would have a high bias.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=43)** You were off by one each time.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=46)** Variance is when the predicted values are scattered all over the place.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=51)** So if you were playing dice and you predicted that you would roll 5 three times, but you actually rolled 2, 4, and 6, then you'd be off by different amounts.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=60)** Then your data would be too spread out.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=63)** Now, it might seem strange to make such a big deal about how the system was wrong, but when you're working with machine learning algorithms, these are two separate challenges.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=72)** So the system needs to fix it in different ways.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=76)** Think about the game of darts.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=78)** The center of the dartboard is the machine's best prediction.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=81)** That means that the little red bullseye in the middle is the right prediction.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=85)** The machine could throw three darts, and each one of them would be consistently wrong.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=90)** They'd all land in the upper right hand corner, just above the red bullseye.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=94)** This is called having a high bias and low variance.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=98)** The darts are grouped together closely, but all of them are too far to the right.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=103)** The dataset would have a high bias.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=106)** That means to make a better prediction, the machine would just have to pull the group of darts down into the left.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=112)** Now imagine a different challenge.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=114)** The machine throws the darts at the dartboard and they're all over the place.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=118)** That means that the data has a wide spread, so this data would have a high variance.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=124)** To make a better prediction, the machine would want to tighten up the darts closest to the bullseye.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=130)** Ideally, you want the predictions to have a low bias and a low variance.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=135)** That means that all the darts are in the bullseye, but in most cases, the machine is going to have to fix either a high bias or a high variance.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=145)** In machine learning, this is such a common problem that it's referred to as the bias variance trade off.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=151)** Like any trade off, it means that the system tries to balance the impact of one, it has to look at the impact on the other.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=158)** So if the machine decreases the variant spread, it will also have to increase the bias.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=164)** If the machine decreases the bias, it will increase the variant spread.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=169)** This is why the machine needs to follow the data.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=172)** The machine will turn each of these knobs to find the best trade off between bias and variance.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/follow-the-data?u=76281980&t=178)** That way it can zero in on the best predictions.

>
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (2)
> **Definitions:** means that (4), is called (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1), throw (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)
> **Speakers:** - in (1)

#### Overfitting and underfitting
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=1)** - When my son was three years old, we told him that he needed to brush his teeth, floss, and take a shower before going to bed.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=7)** Then one day I got a call from his preschool that said he wouldn't take a nap unless they provided him with a shower, toothbrush and flosser.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=15)** I explained to him that it was okay to nap without following those rules.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=19)** He seemed annoyed, but agreed.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=21)** The rules we had created for him were too simple.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=24)** They worked well at home but didn't fit well in the outside world, so we added greater complexity.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=31)** He didn't have to follow the rules for preschool naps, when visiting grandparents, or when flying on an airplane.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=38)** Each time we added more variables, he became more annoyed with the complexity.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=44)** In supervised machine learning, your AI system can run into the same problem.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=48)** The system can create simple rules for its training data that doesn't work well when looking at the larger test data.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=56)** It was like what my son was going through.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=58)** What works well at home doesn't work well in the outside world.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=63)** This challenge is called underfitting the data.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=66)** Sometimes data scientists add more complexity, and then that complexity makes it more difficult for the system to handle.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=74)** This was like all the variables we added to my son's simple rule.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=78)** This is called overfitting the data.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=81)** Imagine that you work for a website like Zillow that matches up buyers and sellers of homes.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=86)** One of the key things that you need to do is estimate the value of the home.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=90)** Your machine could use Naive Bayes to create four predictors, the square footage, the location, number of bathrooms, and the number of bedrooms.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=100)** That way it could look at each predictor independently and then compare it to recently sold houses.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=106)** Then the system would come up with an accurate estimate.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=109)** Now, keep in mind that you're only using four predictors to train your system on how to estimate so the machine is learning from a simple rule.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=118)** It's the same as the simple rule to always shower and brush your teeth before sleeping, so there's a very good chance this rule will underfit the data.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=128)** It's not going to work well when you look at hundreds of thousands of homes.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=132)** On top of that, housing data usually has a lot of variance.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=137)** Remember, that's when your data is spread out.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=139)** There's a lot of homes with different prices that have the same square footage, the same location, and the same number of bathrooms so that makes it difficult to find a close group.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=150)** So to fix this, data scientists can create new predictors.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=155)** Maybe they'll create predictors for quality of view, modern appliances, wood floors, or walkability.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=162)** This creates a much more complex prediction because now your machine needs to balance a lot more predictors.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=170)** So here your rule is overfitting the data.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=173)** The system needs to look at a lot more relationships between these predictors to make an accurate prediction.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=181)** The key thing to keep in mind is there isn't really one way to fix this problem.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=185)** When you're training the system, you need to reach a compromise between simple rules and giving the rules enough complexity to make good predictions.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/overfitting-and-underfitting?u=76281980&t=194)** You need to balance underfitting or overfitting the data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (2), find (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (2)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (2)
> **Code Keywords:** this, (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)
> **Speakers:** - when (1)


### 9. Artificial Neural Networks

> [[#Table of Contents|↑ Back to Table of Contents]]

#### Build a neural network
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=1)** - Machine learning algorithms see patterns in your data, but sometimes you just have too much data to use these algorithms.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=7)** So many large organizations use artificial neural networks instead.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=12)** Artificial neural networks are a type of machine learning that uses a structure like the human brain to break down massive data sets.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=21)** Instead of using the previous machine learning algorithms, an artificial neural network breaks down your data into much smaller pieces.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=29)** Earlier, we talked about artificial neural networks as a machine learning technique that mimics the brain.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=36)** The network is structured with neurons that are organized into layers.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=41)** The layers move from left to right.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=44)** There's the input layer, the hidden layers, and the output layer.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=49)** If the network has a lot of hidden layers, then it's called a deep learning artificial neural network.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=55)** That's because the network is many layers deep.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=58)** The more hidden layers the network has, the easier it'll be for the network to identify very complex patterns.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=64)** So let's imagine creating an artificial neural network to identify whether there's a dog in an image.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=70)** Think of it as a binary classification: dog or not dog.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=75)** To do this, the image from the input layer into dog or not dog needs to be classified.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=82)** You have the image coming in on the input layer, then the output will be the classification into dog or not dog.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=91)** We've already seen that to a machine, an image is just a collection of different bits of data.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=96)** So in this case, you'll have a bunch of pixels.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=99)** These are the tiny points of color on the image and the different levels of brightness or contrast.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=105)** Let's take an image of a dog and break it into pixels.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=109)** Let's say that your image is 25 pixels high and 25 pixels wide.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=114)** So your entire image holds 625 pixels.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=117)** That means that each image has 625 data points.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=122)** Let's say that we take these 625 pixels and feed them into a neural network.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=127)** Each pixel is fed into the input layer.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=130)** Each of the 625 neurons in the input layer has a number based on the color of the pixel.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=136)** Each of the neurons in the hidden layer has something called an activation function.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=141)** An activation function is like a tiny gateway.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=144)** It lets the neuron decide whether it wants to send the data to the next hidden layer in the network.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=149)** Each hidden layer feeds the pixel data forward to the next hidden layer.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=155)** Then at the very end, there'll be two nodes in the output layer.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=158)** Remember, this is a binary classification challenge.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=161)** So there are only two options, is there a dog or not dog?
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=166)** Since the pixel data moves through the layers from left to right, this is called a feed forward neural network.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=174)** One of the great strengths of artificial neural networks is that they're self-tuning.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=178)** They're almost like a musical instrument that tunes themselves until they get the perfect note.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=184)** So each of the two neurons in the output layer will have a probability score.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=189)** The key thing to remember is that an artificial neural network is most often used for supervised learning.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/build-a-neural-network?u=76281980&t=195)** You can train the network and then it will tune itself based on whether it correctly identified your input.

#### Weighing the connections
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=1)** - As human beings, we add weights to our data all the time.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=4)** We look at the features of the data to better predict our output.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=8)** Let's say that you're looking at a photograph of a beautiful grassy open space.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=11)** Then, you see a little blurry object in the photo.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=14)** What do you think the odds are that that blurry object is a dog?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=18)** Now, imagine you're looking at an image of a dry desert.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=22)** This picture also has a little blurry object.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=24)** What do you think the odds are that this object is a dog?
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=28)** If you're like most people, you'd guess that a dog is much more likely to be in a grassy field, so your human neural connections added a positive weight to the grassy field and a negative way to the arid desert.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=41)** Artificial neural networks do the same thing.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=44)** Like us, these networks need to work in a world of probabilities.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=48)** It's possible that there's a dog in the middle of the desert, but if you're an artificial neuron, you're going to be very skeptical about activating.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=57)** An artificial neural network is structured in a way so that it can better tune itself to understand your data.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=63)** It's almost like a self-tuning musical instrument.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=66)** To tune an instrument like a guitar, you need knobs to twist as you strum the note.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=72)** With artificial neural networks, these knobs change the weights of your connections between your neurons.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=80)** An artificial neuron network adds weights to the connections between neurons in each layer.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=86)** Each neuron in the hidden layer feeds forward into every other neuron in the next layer.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=92)** So if there are a hundred neurons in every hidden layer, each neuron in that layer will have a hundred connections going out.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=100)** That's a lot of connectivity, but where it gets really powerful is that each one of these connections will have a weight.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=107)** That's why if you've ever seen a sketch of a neural network, you'll see that each one of the connection lines has a W with a number.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=114)** So in this case, you would have a W1, W2, W3, all the way up to W100.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=121)** You'd see this for each one of the hidden layers.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=124)** Now, the weights in each one of these connections is a key part of how an artificial neural network tunes itself.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=132)** Keep in mind, an artificial neural network is just a form of supervised machine learning.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=137)** So data scientists use the same technique that they've used to train the network.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=141)** Remember that supervised machine learning starts out with a training set.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=146)** Then once the algorithm is tuned to make accurate predictions, you can then move on to the larger test data set.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=152)** The same thing happens with your artificial neural network.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=156)** When you first initialize your neural network, the systems will randomly assign numbers to these thousands of weights.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=163)** Then you'll feed your training data into the network and let the system adjust the weights based on whether you're getting correct output.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=170)** The network will repeat this over and over again until it's accurately identifying the patterns for the output.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/weighing-the-connections?u=76281980&t=177)** It'll tune itself over time to zero in on the best predictions.

#### The activation bias
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=1)** - An artificial neural network is self-tuning.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=4)** You've seen it's like a musical instrument.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=6)** It compares the output of the perfect note and then twists its own dials to match the sound.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=12)** But at the end of the day, an artificial neural network is still a form of machine learning.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=17)** That means it uses many of the same tools and techniques to help the system learn.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=22)** You've already seen that an artificial neural network tunes itself by adding weights to the connections, but adding weights to these connections only corrects the variance.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=33)** Remember earlier that the system is trying to throw darts in a tight cluster near the bullseye.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=39)** The network will throw a dart and then measure how close it is to making the right prediction.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=44)** Then it will adjust the weights and throw another dart to see if it's closer.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=49)** Remember that when you're making a prediction, you need to balance the bias and variance in the data.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=55)** This is called the bias-variance tradeoff.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=58)** So adjusting the variance will have an impact on the bias.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=62)** In an artificial neural network, the bias is the number that the system assigns to each neuron.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=68)** This bias number will shift the data in a different direction to make it more accurate.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=75)** The network must tune itself to find a sweet spot between the data bias and the data variance.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=81)** The main dial that it has to tune itself is adding weights to the connections and adding a bias to the neuron.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=89)** Sometimes you almost feel bad for making the artificial neural network go through this tuning process.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=94)** It adjusts the weights of its connections to decrease the variant spread, but that shifts it slightly away from the target.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=101)** Then it adds a bias to correct for the shift, but then that makes the data spread out again.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=106)** Humans would find this very frustrating.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=108)** It's like the machine is trying to throw darts in a tight formation while the same time using bias to shift the whole dartboard closer to the bullseye.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=117)** On top of that, artificial neural networks tend to overfit the data.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=122)** Remember that overfitting is when the system adds a lot of complexity when it's training.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=127)** So when an artificial neural network is looking at the data in a training set, it might overlearn lessons about the data.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=134)** Since it's overfitting its training set, it might make big shifts when you adjust the variance.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=139)** That makes it even more difficult for the machine to find a nice balance between the bias and the variance.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=145)** It's like the system is trying to drive straight on an icy road, on one side of the road is too much bias, and on the other side of the road is too much variance.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=154)** If it slides too much in one direction, it has to steer its way back.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=159)** One key thing to remember about bias is that it's on the neuron and not assigned to the connection like the weights.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=166)** If you think about it, that makes a lot of sense.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=168)** The machine can only add the bias after it sees what happens with the variance.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=173)** In a sense, it can only shift the dartboard after it's already thrown a few darts.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/the-activation-bias?u=76281980&t=178)** Otherwise, the machine wouldn't know which way to make the shifts.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (3)
> **Code Keywords:** throw (4), self (1)
> **Analogies:** it's like (3)
> **Definitions:** is called (1)
> **Speakers:** - an (1)


### 10. Improve Accuracy

> [[#Table of Contents|↑ Back to Table of Contents]]

#### Learning from mistakes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=1)** - We humans tend to think of right and wrong as one or the other.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=4)** You're either right or you're wrong.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=6)** But artificial neural networks need to be much more specific.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=10)** To a neural network, 95% right is much different from 97% right.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=16)** The challenge is how the system figures out how wrong that is.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=21)** In a sense, the neural network needs a measure of wrongness.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=26)** In neural networks, this is measured as a cost function.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=30)** The cost function is just a number that the system uses to measure its answer against the correct answer.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=37)** If it's really close, then that number will be small.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=40)** But if it's really far off, then that number will be larger.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=44)** Say your neural network is trying to determine if an image contains a dog.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=48)** The network says there's a 97% chance that it's a dog photo, but it turns out that it's a cat photo.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=56)** That wrongness will have a slight cost.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=60)** Now let's say that the network says that there's a 99% chance that it's a dog photo, but this time, it's a photo of a snow-covered mountain.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=68)** This wrongness will have a much higher cost.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=71)** That's because here, the network was very, very wrong, so it needs to make more aggressive adjustments to its weights and biases.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=82)** Trying to correct for wrongness is a tricky thing.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=85)** For that, a lot of neural networks use something called gradient descent.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=90)** Gradient means steepness and descent means going down.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=95)** So imagine that your artificial neural network is playing darts again.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=98)** It's making predictions and seeing how close it gets to the bullseye.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=102)** Some of the predictions are way off, but other predictions are close.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=107)** When it throws a dart, there's a distance between it and the dart board.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=111)** When it's in the air, the dart travels at an upward angle and then a downward angle.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=117)** Then it hits the board.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=119)** If it misses the board entirely, it'll want to make a bigger change to the angle.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=124)** If it's very close to the target, then it'll want to make the tiniest change to the angle.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=129)** That way, it can hit the bullseye.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=131)** Well, the neural network does pretty much the same thing.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=134)** It uses calculations of gradient descent to adjust the weights and biases in the network.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=140)** It's not using darts, but it's using a very similar calculation to measure the angle of its wrongness.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=147)** In fact, this is one of the biggest innovations in artificial neural networks.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=152)** It's called the back propagation of errors in the network, or backprop for short.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=158)** Remember, we're using what's called a feedforward artificial network.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=162)** Your data goes from left to right.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=165)** It starts at the input layer and moves forward to the output layer.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=169)** But when your network makes a mistake, it needs to go backwards.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=173)** It needs to use the gradient descent to determine its wrongness.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=178)** Then it will use backprop to adjust the weights and biases based on the seriousness of its error.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=185)** If the network really overshoots the target, it'll want to make extreme adjustments.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=190)** But if the prediction is very close, then the network should be much more careful.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=195)** The network will feed forward and then correct backward.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/learning-from-mistakes?u=76281980&t=198)** That way, it can tune itself towards the correct answer.

> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)

#### Step through the network
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=1)** - So what does it take to build an AI system?
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=3)** To think about this, let's go back to our challenge of finding dogs in images.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=8)** The first step data scientists need to do is figure out what they want from the data.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=13)** In this case, they're not asking the AI system to cluster together its own groups, instead, they're asking the system to classify data into two categories.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=23)** One category will have images with dogs, and the others will be not dogs.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=29)** This is a classic binary classification challenge.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=33)** Remember, that's when the neural network just has two possible classifications.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=38)** This means they'll be doing supervised machine learning.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=41)** Remember that supervised machine learning starts out with labeled data.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=46)** Here, the system will be trained with hundreds of thousands of images known to contain dogs.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=52)** The next step for data scientists is to figure out if they want to use standard machine learning algorithms or if they'd like to use an artificial neural network.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=61)** Remember that this is a classification problem, so if they go with machine learning algorithms, they'll probably either use K nearest neighbor or naive Bayes.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=71)** You've seen that the system will break down each image into pixels.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=75)** That means that this is going to be a complex challenge with a lot of data, so they're going to use an artificial neural network.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=82)** They'll create the input layer, hidden layers, and output layer.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=86)** Now, remember that since this is a binary classification challenge, there are only two options: dog or not dog, so they'll need just two nodes for the output layer.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=98)** Next, the neural network will be initialized.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=101)** The system will assign random numbers to all the weights of the connections.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=106)** Then the system will set the bias on all the nodes to zero.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=110)** This is almost like shaking an Etch A Sketch to give itself a clean slate.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=115)** Now, the training set needs to be fed into the neural network.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=119)** The first few images will probably be not that much better than random guesses.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=123)** The neural network will say something like: there's a 62% chance that the image contains a dog, or a 55% chance it's not.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=132)** Then the network will compare its answer to the label on the data.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=136)** If it mis-identifies the dog image, then it will look at the gradient descent to determine how much to change the weights and biases.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=146)** The neural network will go through all the data in your training set to fine tune its results.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=152)** Remember that the network will tune itself by using back propagation to change the weights and bias to lower the cost function.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=162)** In a sense, it will go backward through the network and twist all the dials to increase its accuracy.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=169)** Once the artificial neural network has gone through the training set, the data will be added from the test set.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=175)** The test set will not be labeled.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=177)** It could be hundreds of thousands of images of anything.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=181)** Then you'll see how well your neural network perform when identifying dog photos.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=186)** Sometimes the neural network will do very well with the training set, but not so well with the test set.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=192)** When this happens, it usually means that you are under-fitting the data.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=197)** Remember, that's when the system's really good at identifying the smaller training set, but doesn't have enough complexity to deal with the new data in the test set.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=207)** Now, it's unlikely that your team will classify dog or not dog anytime soon.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=212)** However, this approach to binary classification is a powerful way to get insights from your data.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/step-through-the-network?u=76281980&t=218)** Think about your customer data, your sales data, or even data from your IOT devices, and what you can learn from this approach.

### 11. The Rise of Generative AI

> [[#Table of Contents|↑ Back to Table of Contents]]

#### Self-supervised learning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=0)** - In 1798, Napoleon Bonaparte invaded Egypt.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=4)** In the small town of Rosetta, the French army rediscovered a stone with three ancient languages.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=12)** When the French lost Egypt to the British, the soldiers took this large Rosetta Stone back to London.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=19)** The stone was unique because it contained Greek translations of ancient Egyptian hieroglyphs.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=25)** This was a terrific discovery because there were no labels of ancient Egyptian hieroglyphs.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=32)** Since many historians knew Greek, they were able to then label these hieroglyphs with a known language.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=39)** The British historians looked at the data they knew and then used it as a guide to make guesses about data they didn't know.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=47)** Those guesses became the foundation for understanding Egyptian hieroglyphs.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=53)** It might sound strange, but modern generative AI systems use a similar technique called self-supervised learning.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=61)** Remember that most of the data in the world is unstructured.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=65)** That means that there's no label to understand the data.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=69)** So gen AI systems need to compare this unlabeled data with data it already knows.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=75)** In a sense, it makes guesses about the data based on the contents.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=79)** Then it will label this data based on these guesses.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=83)** Gen AI uses unsupervised learning to analyze this data and generate pseudo labels.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=90)** These are called pseudo labels because they're just guesses made by the machine.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=94)** These pseudo labels allow it to be used with supervised learning.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=99)** Imagine creating a self-supervised system to label all available dog pictures.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=104)** This could be billions of images.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=106)** Most of these images won't be labeled.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=108)** That means that most won't have a caption that says, image of a dog.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=112)** Instead, they'll just be included in a social media post where someone says something like out for morning walk.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=119)** The first thing the self-supervised learning system does is to use unsupervised learning to cluster similar images together.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=127)** So maybe most of them are outside.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=129)** It'll likely pick up on patterns of images that contain fur.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=133)** It might pick up on a pattern in the dog's tail or ears.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=137)** Then it will use another form of unsupervised learning to see if it can find other data.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=143)** That data will act as a guide to help it make a guess.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=148)** It will be the Rosetta Stone that the system uses to label the image as a dog.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=154)** Maybe it will find common patterns and captions that say something like walk, outside, and park.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=162)** It'll then make a guess that images near these captions are more likely to contain a dog.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=168)** Then it will allow the system to create pseudo labels for billions of dog images that would otherwise be buried in all that unstructured data.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=177)** With these pseudo labels, the supervised learning system will be able to classify these dog images based on what it learns.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=185)** That means that you could have classifications based on dog breeds or even their height and weight.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=192)** Now, it's important to keep in mind that self-supervised learning solves a specific problem.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=197)** It's about getting value from massive amounts of unstructured data.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=201)** It vacuums up so much data that there aren't many companies that have the computing power to process it all.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/self-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=208)** Even though most companies probably won't be building their own self-supervised systems, the ones that do will get an enormous learning power from this approach.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Code Keywords:** self (5)
> **Definitions:** means that (3)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Foundation models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - So now you've seen that self-supervised learning is a powerful new machine learning technique.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=6)** It's like a giant vacuum that sucks up all the data in the world and gives it an accurate label.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=12)** It does this without very much human intervention.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=15)** This technique moves beyond data models and predictive artificial intelligence.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=20)** That's why this is the primary technique that you use to create generative AI systems.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=26)** Remember that generative AI systems are much more flexible.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=30)** When you ask a generative AI chatbot a question, it needs to have wide knowledge of many different topics.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=37)** It needs to be able to connect birds, cars, television shows, and a bunch of other data.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=44)** In the past, you would have to retrain your model anytime you added new data.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=49)** That's why in 2021, a group at Stanford University came up with a new way to think about models.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=56)** Data models were fine tuned for a specific task.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=60)** So instead, they proposed creating foundation models that could do a wide range of tasks without retraining on new data.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=69)** So think about how this might work with self-supervised learning.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=72)** Imagine that you wanted to vacuum up all the data from the United States Library of Congress.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=78)** You could use unsupervised learning to cluster together different ideas and topics.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=83)** You can almost think of this as a similar stuff cluster.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=88)** These could be people, places, and things that are somehow interconnected.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=93)** Imagine that you had an unsupervised cluster of information about ancient sculptures.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=100)** You could then use a form of supervised learning to label this cluster for your chatbot.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=105)** Since they're not human-created labels, they're called pseudo labels.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=110)** These pseudo labels might include the words Greek, marble, Acropolis, Florence, Michelangelo, and Pheidias.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=118)** The machine could potentially create billions of these pseudo labels.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=123)** It could also vacuum up all the data on something like French fries.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=128)** Then the system could create pseudo labels for crinkle cut, Belgian, steak fries, and even fast food restaurants.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=135)** Now this self-supervised learning system could build its foundation model.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=139)** It could have billions of pseudo labels with trillions of bits of data.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=144)** This foundation model gives the AI system a much wider view of the world.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=150)** That's how you can use these GenAI systems to create a marble sculpture of a French fry.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=155)** It has the pseudo labels and the data it needs to make an accurate image.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=160)** That's also why these generative AI chat systems do something called hallucinate.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=165)** This is when the system says something that sounds like it could be accurate, but it isn't true.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=170)** It might say that George Washington went to the University of Wisconsin, or that you should eat pebbles to improve your health.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=178)** What's happening that the system is creating billions of these pseudo labels.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=182)** After vacuuming up all that data, it mismatched a connection.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=187)** Maybe a professor from the University of Wisconsin wrote a great book on George Washington.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=192)** Then the system made a mismatch.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=194)** Now, the key thing to keep in mind is that foundation models are completely different from data models.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=200)** In fact, foundation models can sometimes be used to make predictions, but data models can't be used to make generative AI systems.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-artificial-intelligence-24947908/foundation-models?u=76281980&t=209)** Foundation models are powerful and flexible, and data models are streamlined but trained for just one task.
